<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The emerging biofuel crop Camelina sativa retains a highly undifferentiated hexaploid genome structure"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-04-23"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Camelina sativa is an oilseed with desirable agronomic and oil-quality attributes for a viable industrial oil platform crop. Here we generate the first chromosome-scale high-quality reference genome sequence for C. sativa and annotated 89,418 protein-coding genes, representing a whole-genome triplication event relative to the crucifer model Arabidopsis thaliana. C. sativa represents the first crop species to be sequenced from lineage I of the Brassicaceae. The well-preserved hexaploid genome structure of C. sativa surprisingly mirrors those of economically important amphidiploid Brassica crop species from lineage II as well as wheat and cotton. The three genomes of C. sativa show no evidence of fractionation bias and limited expression-level bias, both characteristics commonly associated with polyploid evolution. The highly undifferentiated polyploid genome of C. sativa presents significant consequences for breeding and genetic manipulation of this industrial oil crop. Camelina sativa is an oilseed crop with important industrial applications. Here, the authors sequence the C. sativagenome to investigate the genome organization and evolution of this species, and to provide a valuable tool for genetic engineering and potential crop improvement.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Camelina sativa</i> is an oilseed with desirable agronomic and oil-quality attributes for a viable industrial oil platform crop. Here we generate the first chromosome-scale high-quality reference genome sequence for <i>C. sativa</i> and annotated 89,418 protein-coding genes, representing a whole-genome triplication event relative to the crucifer model <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. <i>C. sativa</i> represents the first crop species to be sequenced from lineage I of the Brassicaceae. The well-preserved hexaploid genome structure of <i>C. sativa</i> surprisingly mirrors those of economically important amphidiploid <i>Brassica</i> crop species from lineage II as well as wheat and cotton. The three genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> show no evidence of fractionation bias and limited expression-level bias, both characteristics commonly associated with polyploid evolution. The highly undifferentiated polyploid genome of <i>C. sativa</i> presents significant consequences for breeding and genetic manipulation of this industrial oil crop.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>C<i>amelina sativa</i> (false flax or gold of pleasure) is a relict oilseed crop of the Crucifer family (Brassicaceae) with centres of origin in southeastern Europe and southwestern Asia. <i>C. sativa</i> was cultivated in Europe as an important oilseed crop for many centuries before being displaced by higher-yielding crops such as canola (<i>Brassica napus</i>) and wheat. <i>C. sativa</i> has several agronomic advantages for production, including early maturity, low requirement for water and nutrients, adaptability to adverse environmental conditions and resistance to common cruciferous pests and pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gehringer, A., Friedt, W., Luhs, W. &amp; Snowdon, R. J. Genetic mapping of agronomic traits in false flax (Camelina sativa subsp. sativa). Genome 49, 1555–1563 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d22669e577">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moser, B. R. Biodiesel from alternative oilseed feedstocks: camelina and field pennycress. Biofuels 3, 193–209 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22669e580">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Séguin-Swartz, G. et al. Diseases of Camelina sativa (false flax). Can. J. Plant Pathol. 31, 375–386 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d22669e583">3</a></sup>. With seed oil content (36–47%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moser, B. R. Biodiesel from alternative oilseed feedstocks: camelina and field pennycress. Biofuels 3, 193–209 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22669e588">2</a></sup> twice that of soybean (18–22%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moser, B. R. Biodiesel from alternative oilseed feedstocks: camelina and field pennycress. Biofuels 3, 193–209 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22669e592">2</a></sup> and a fatty acid profile (with &gt;90% unsaturated fatty acids) suitable for making jet fuel, biodiesel and high-value industrial lubricants, <i>C. sativa</i> has tremendous potential to serve as a viable and renewable feedstock for multiple industries. Additionally, due to exceptionally high levels of α-linolenic acid (32–40% of total oil content)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moser, B. R. Biodiesel from alternative oilseed feedstocks: camelina and field pennycress. Biofuels 3, 193–209 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22669e599">2</a></sup>, <i>C. sativa</i> oil offers an additional source of essential fatty acids. The residual essential fatty acids combined with low glucosinolate levels in <i>C. sativa</i> meal make it desirable as an animal feed. Considering the broad applications, <i>C. sativa</i> is currently being re-embraced as an industrial oil platform crop; however, due to limited availability of genetic and genomic resources, the full agronomic and breeding potential of this emerging oilseed crop remains largely unexploited.</p><p>Genetically, <i>C. sativa</i> is closely related to the model plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (lineage I of the Brassicaceae) and more distantly to the important vegetable oilseed crop, canola (lineage II)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Beilstein, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Mathews, S. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Brassicaceae phylogeny inferred from phytochrome A and ndhF sequence data: tribes and trichomes revisited. Am. J. Bot. 95, 1307–1327 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22669e622">4</a></sup>. The previously estimated genome size (750 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hutcheon, C. et al. Polyploid genome of Camelina sativa revealed by isolation of fatty acid synthesis genes. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 233 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22669e626">5</a></sup> and chromosome count (<i>n</i>=20) of <i>C. sativa</i> are higher compared with most of the Brassicaceae species that have been sequenced to date (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). However, the genetic basis for the genome expansion of <i>C. sativa</i> is currently unknown. In many plant taxa, polyploidization and proliferation of transposable elements (TEs) are recognized as prevalent factors in plant genome expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bennetzen, J. L. Mechanisms and rates of genome expansion and contraction in flowering plants. Genetica 115, 29–36 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d22669e643">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kumar, A. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Plant retrotransposons. Annu. Rev. Genet. 33, 479–532 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22669e646">7</a></sup>. Similar to the diploid progenitors of canola (<i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d22669e657">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Segmental structure of the Brassica napus genome based on comparative analysis with Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 171, 765–781 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d22669e660">9</a></sup>, <i>C. sativa</i> is suggested to have undergone a genome triplication event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hutcheon, C. et al. Polyploid genome of Camelina sativa revealed by isolation of fatty acid synthesis genes. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 233 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22669e667">5</a></sup>. However, the evolutionary origin and mode of the polyploidization event that formed the <i>C. sativa</i> genome as well as the post-polyploidization evolutionary path leading to its diploidization are currently not understood.</p><p>Here we sequence a homozygous doubled haploid line of <i>C. sativa</i> and assembled 82% of the estimated genome size in order to decipher the genome organization of <i>C. sativa</i> and facilitate development of genetic and genomic tools essential for crop improvement. The genome sequence of <i>C. sativa</i> will provide an indispensable tool for genetic manipulation and further crop improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The genome of a homozygous doubled haploid line of <i>C. sativa</i> (DH55) was sequenced using a hybrid Illumina and Roche 454 next-generation sequencing (NGS) approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Filtered sequence data (96.53 Gb) provided 123 × coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 1</a>) of the estimated genome size of 785 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 2</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), which was assembled using a hierarchical assembly strategy (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 1</a>) into 37,871 scaffolds, with a sequence span of 641.45 Mb and an N50 size of 2.16 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 2</a>). A high-density genetic map based on 3,575 polymorphic markers allowed 608.54 Mb of the assembled genome, represented by 588 scaffolds to be anchored to the 20 chromosomes of <i>C. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 3</a>), thereby producing a highly contiguous final assembly with an N50 size of &gt;30 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The final genome assembly contains 641.45 Mb of sequence, covering 82% of the estimated genome size, 95% of which is in 20 chromosomes. A summary describing the overall features as well as completeness and contiguity of the genome assembly is provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 4</a>. Comparison of the genome sequence with a set of independently assembled BAC scaffolds, expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and core eukaryotic genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 3</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Tables 5–8</a>) confirmed the quality as well as near complete coverage of the euchromatic space and gene complement in the assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The Camelina sativa genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The <i>Camelina sativa</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="678" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>From the outside ring to the centre: (1) the twenty <i>C. sativa</i> pseudochromosomes (Chr1–20 represented on Mb scale) are shown in different colours with putative centromeric regions indicated by black bands; (2) gene expression levels (log10(average FPKM), bin=250 Kb)), values range from 0 (yellow) to 3.92 (red); (3) the distribution of protein-coding regions (nucleotides per 500 Kb; orange) compared with repetitive sequences (nucleotides per 500 Kb; yellow); and (4) <i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i> ratios (median, bin=500 kb) of syntenic (blue) and non-syntenic (green) genes. The centre shows a graphical view of the triplicated segments of annotated genes connected with lines of colours matching those for the pseudochromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Repeat annotation revealed that 28% (180.12 Mb) of the assembled <i>C. sativa</i> genome comprises TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Retrotransposons were found to be the dominant class of repeat elements (19%), while DNA transposons accounted for 3% of the genome. Similar to most higher plant genomes, repetitive elements in <i>C. sativa</i> were more abundant in the vicinity of centromeres and less so in gene-dense regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The genome occupancy of repetitive DNA in <i>C. sativa</i> (28%) is comparable to the low abundance of TEs in <i>A. thaliana</i> (24%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22669e805">10</a></sup> and <i>A. lyrata</i> (30%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22669e812">10</a></sup>. However, it is much smaller than in <i>B. rapa</i> (39.5%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kumar, A. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Plant retrotransposons. Annu. Rev. Genet. 33, 479–532 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22669e819">7</a></sup> as well as other similar-sized plant genomes, including potato (62%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d22669e824">11</a></sup>, soybean (59%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d22669e828">12</a></sup> and sorghum (62%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d22669e832">13</a></sup>. Thus, unlike <i>B. rapa</i> and other angiosperm species, genome expansion in <i>C. sativa</i> has not resulted from repetitive sequence proliferation.</p><p>RNA-seq data (78.5 Gb) was generated from tissue samples collected at 12 different growth stages to assist with annotation of protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Based on a comprehensive strategy of <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction and homology evidence from proteome data sets, ESTs and RNA-seq transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), 89,418 non-redundant <i>C. sativa</i> genes were predicted, of which 4,753 (5.3%) genes encoded two or more alternatively spliced isoforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 4</a>). More than 95% (85,274) of these annotated genes were located on the pseudochromosomes with the remainder on unanchored scaffolds. The overall gene model characteristics, such as gene length and exon-intron structures are comparable to other Brassicaceae species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). The genome composition (genic, intergenic and repeat regions) of <i>C. sativa</i> is more similar to that of <i>A. lyrata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22669e869">10</a></sup> than <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Based on sequence identity a total of 86,849 (97.13%) of the predicted <i>C. sativa</i> genes have homologues in the UniProt database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Data 1</a>), and RNA-seq evidence suggested that &gt;90% of the genes were expressed (FPKM&gt;0) in one or more developmental stages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The genome sequence and its annotation are available along with a genome browser at <a href="http://www.camelinadb.ca">http://www.camelinadb.ca</a>.</p><p>The predicted number of protein-coding genes in <i>C. sativa</i> is significantly higher than other currently sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Interestingly, the gene number is similar to that predicted for bread wheat whose genome is almost 22 times larger than that of <i>C. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature 491, 705–710 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d22669e910">14</a></sup>. The estimated total number of genes in <i>C. sativa</i> is approximately three times that of the model <i>Arabidopsis</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 11</a>), suggesting that the <i>C. sativa</i> genome resulted from a whole-genome triplication of a common ancestor. To determine whether the expanded gene repertoire in <i>C. sativa</i> could have arisen from an expansion of lineage-specific <i>C. sativa</i> orphan genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Dujon, B. The yeast genome project: what did we learn? Trends Genet. 12, 263–270 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d22669e933">15</a></sup>, we used a BLAST-based filtering approach comparing <i>C. sativa</i> genes with all sequenced plant taxa excluding the five Brassicaceae species. A total of 3,761 Brassicaceae-specific orphan genes were identified, of which 1,656 were <i>C. sativa</i>–specific, which accounts for only 1.85% of annotated protein-coding genes in <i>C. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 4</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Tables 13 and 14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of gene number in C. sativa plotted against genome size with a subset of completely sequenced plant genomes (N=35)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Comparison of gene number in <i>C. sativa</i> plotted against genome size with a subset of completely sequenced plant genomes (<i>N</i>=35).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="409" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The significance test for a single outlier in regression data was performed as described in detail in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 8</a> and the confidence intervals for the regression line are shown as dotted lines. Mes, <i>Manihot esculenta</i>; Rco, <i>Ricinus communis</i>; Lus, <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>; Ptr, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>; Mtr, <i>Medicago truncatula</i>; Pvu, <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>; Gma, <i>Glycine max</i>; Csa, <i>Cucumis sativis</i>; Ppe, <i>Prunus persica</i>; Mdo, <i>Malus domestica</i>; Fve, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>; Ath, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>; Aly, <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>; Csa, <i>Camelina sativa</i>; Cru, <i>Capsella rubella</i>; Bra, <i>Brassica rapa</i>; Tha, <i>Thellungiella halophila</i>; Cpa, <i>Carica papaya</i>; Gra, <i>Gossypium raimondii</i>; Tca, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>; Csi, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>; Egr, <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i>; Vvi, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>; Stu, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>; Sly, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>; Mgu, <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>; Aco, <i>Aquilegia coerulea</i>; Sbi, <i>Sorghum bicolour</i>; Zma, <i>Zea mays</i>; Sit, <i>Setaria italica</i>; Pvi, <i>Panicum virgatum</i>; Osa, <i>Oryza sativa</i>; Bdi, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>; Smo, <i>Selaginella moellendorfii</i>; Ppa, <i>Physcomitrella patens</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Synteny and collinearity with Brassicaceae species</h3><p>The genome sequence and gene annotations of <i>C. sativa</i> were compared with those of phylogenetically closely related Brassicaceae species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>), including <i>A. thaliana</i> (model Crucifer species), <i>A. lyrata</i> (reference for ancestral karyotype) and <i>B. rapa</i> (reference for polyploidization). Chromosomal collinearity assessed through whole-genome alignments revealed a striking level of conservation between the genome sequence of <i>C. sativa</i> and the two <i>Arabidopsis</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). The longest stretches of conserved syntenic blocks were observed between the <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), with syntenic regions spanning almost complete chromosomes from both of these species. Notably, every chromosome or chromosomal region in <i>A. lyrata</i> or <i>A. thaliana</i> was represented in three independent chromosomes in the <i>C. sativa</i> genome, thus providing robust evidence for a whole-genome triplication event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparative analysis and evolution of the C. sativa genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparative analysis and evolution of the <i>C. sativa</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="875" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) MUMer plot comparing the <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> genomes. Syntenic and collinear regions making the three complete sub-genomes in <i>C. sativa</i> are circled in red, blue and green. (<b>b</b>) Reconstruction of the three sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa.</i> Chromosome and ancestral genomic-block-level organization of the sub-genomes in <i>C. sativa</i> is shown. Based on synteny and collinearity between <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> species, and GB contiguity in the ancestral karyotype, pseudochromosomes were assigned to three sub-genomes in <i>C. sativa</i>. Each pseudochromosome was subdivided among ancestral genomic blocks (A–X), which are coloured based on their occurrence in the ACK. (<b>c</b>) ACK consisting of the 24 conserved genomic blocks (A–X). (<b>d</b>) The ancestral diploid karyotype (derivative of ACK) of <i>C. sativa</i>. (<b>e</b>) The presumed origin and reconstruction of the fusion chromosome (AK2/4) of the dACK.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Reconstructing the three sub-genomes of hexaploid <i>C. sativa</i></h3><p>The triplicated chromosomal segments in <i>C. sativa</i> were identified and assigned to a sub-genome using the protein-coding genes from <i>A. thaliana</i> as discrete genomic anchors to determine the corresponding syntenic orthologues (syntelogs) from <i>C. sativa</i> and the extent of the collinear conserved block environment. A syntelog matrix representing individual <i>A. thaliana</i> genes and the corresponding triplets of <i>C. sativa</i> homologues is presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Data 2</a>. A total of 62,277 <i>C. sativa</i> genes were found to be syntenically orthologous to <i>A. thaliana</i> genes; these genes will be referred to as ‘syntelogs’, and the remaining 27,141 genes are divided into tandem duplicates (10,792 genes) and ‘non-syntenic genes’ (16,349 genes). Syntelogs were further classified as either ‘fully retained’ (if all three homologues were retained) or ‘fractionated’ (if one or two of the homologues were lost).</p><p>Comparative mapping and cytogenetic studies have suggested that most of the Brassicaceae species have evolved from an ancestral karyotype comprising 8 chromosomes and 24 conserved genomic blocks (labelled as A–X)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC’s of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1256">16</a></sup>. To decipher the nature of the ancestral karyotype of <i>C. sativa</i>, its genomic block (GB) structure was elucidated based on previously defined GB intervals in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1265">17</a></sup>. As expected, for each <i>A. thaliana</i> GB three syntenic copies were detected in <i>C. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Of the 24 GBs, 20 were found to be maintained in three nearly undisrupted copies, whereas the remaining four GBs (D, E, I and J) exhibited rearrangements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>Utilizing the extensive synteny and collinearity between <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> species, GB contiguity in the ancestral karyotype, and the assumption that syntenic fragments of each <i>C. sativa</i> chromosome derive from the same ancestral chromosome, the most parsimonious path to sub-genome structure was deduced (discussed in Methods) and the triplicated sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i> were reconstructed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Accordingly, sub-genome I of <i>C. sativa</i> (Cs-G1) contains six chromosomes, while the other two sub-genomes (Cs-G2 and Cs-G3) contain seven chromosomes each (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). The sub-genomes each encode 28,274, 27,218 and 29,207 genes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 15</a>), which are highly comparable to the <i>A. thaliana</i> gene complement.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Deciphering the ancestral karyotype of <i>C. sativa</i></h3><p>The organization of the GBs in <i>C. sativa</i> was compared with previously inferred ancestral karyotypes, including the ancestral crucifer karyotype (ACK; <i>n</i>=8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC’s of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1331">16</a></sup>, proto-calepineae karyotype (PCK; <i>n</i>=7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Mandakova, T. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Chromosomal phylogeny and karyotype evolution in x=7 crucifer species (Brassicaceae). Plant Cell 20, 2559–2570 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1338">18</a></sup> and translocated PCK (tPCK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1343">17</a></sup>; <i>n</i>=7; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 16</a>). All 16 GB associations that were defined in ACK were conserved in <i>C. sativa</i> but only 11 of 17 GB associations defined for PCK and tPCK were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 16</a>), suggesting that <i>C. sativa</i> genome organization is more similar to the ACK (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). However, a number of additional unique GB associations observed in <i>C. sativa</i>, such as D/I, E/I, N/J, Q/V, O/W and O/R have not been reported in ancestral Brassicaceae karyotypes defined so far. Only two of these novel GB associations D/I and E/I were common to all three sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Additionally, Csa16, Csa7 and Csa5/9 from Cs-G1, Cs-G2 and Cs-G3, respectively, carrying these two novel GB associations display further rearrangements resulting in a common organization of GBs as J/I/D/E/I/E/I/D (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), suggesting the pre-existence of this novel GB structure in the parental karyotype from which the three sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> evolved.</p><p>Based on the above observations, the putative diploid karyotype of <i>C. sativa</i>, named dACK (derivative of ACK), comprising seven chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>) was inferred. The dACK karyotype (<i>n</i>=7) comprises six ancestral chromosomes (AK1, 3, 5, 6, 7 and 8) and a chromosomal fusion (AK2/4) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Previous karyotype analyses of <i>C. sativa</i> and related <i>Camelina</i> species has detailed a range of chromosome numbers including <i>n</i>=6, 7, 13 and 20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Koch, M. A. et al. BrassiBase: tools and biological resources to study characters and traits in the Brassicaceae-version 1.1. Taxon 61, 1001–1009 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1411">19</a>). The lower chromosome numbers are consistent with the dACK karyotype and the identified sub-genomes. The higher chromosome counts could suggest that two independent hybridization events resulted in the current hexaploid genome. However, without reference to extant diploid relatives of each sub-genome it is difficult to accurately determine the origin of <i>C. sativa</i>. EST contigs derived from 454 pyrosequencing of the leaf transcriptome of five representatives of all known lower chromosome number <i>Camelina</i> species were used to derive the phylogenetic relationship between these species and the three sub-genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 5</a>). The genomes of Cs-G1 and Cs-G2 are more closely related to each other than any of the diploids assayed, which could suggest an initial tetraploidisation event of two closely related species (that is, possibly an amphidiploid), subsequently followed by an additional hybridization event through which Cs-G3 joined, resulting in a hexaploid genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenetic relationship between the three sub-genomes of C. sativa and lower-chromosome Camelina species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Phylogenetic relationship between the three sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> and lower-chromosome <i>Camelina</i> species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="193" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>A maximum-likelihood tree produced from a supermatrix constructed using 4,867 orthologous sequences. Clade support values near nodes represent bootstrap proportions in percentages. Branch lengths represent estimated nucleotide substitutions per site.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of the distribution of synonymous substitutions (<i>K</i>s) among the coding regions of paralogous gene pairs provided an estimate of the age of divergence of the three sub-genomes in <i>C. sativa</i>. Mixture model analysis of the <i>K</i>s distribution uncovered the previously documented Brassicaceae-related α, β and γ paleopolyploidy events, and revealed the presence of an additional peak at <i>K</i>s=~0.09 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Assuming an established synonymous substitution rate of 8.22 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions/synonymous site/year for Brassicaceae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Beilstein, M. A., Nagalingum, N. S., Clements, M. D., Manchester, S. R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724–18728 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1478">20</a></sup>, the three genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> were estimated to have separated ~5.41 million years ago (Mya), which is comparable to the divergence time of the three mesopolyploid (functionally diploid) <i>Brassica</i> genomes (<i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. nigra</i>) that fused in all pairwise combinations to form the allopolyploid crop species <i>B. napus</i> (canola), <i>B. juncea</i> (oriental mustard) and <i>B. carinata</i> (Ethiopian mustard)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of Brassica napus and peculiar mode of fertilization. Jpn J. Bot 7, 389–452 (1935)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1507">21</a></sup>. The mesopolyploid structure of the <i>Brassica</i> diploid genomes exhibit extensive reduction of chromosome number and rearrangement of ancestral chromosomal blocks. This structure is also mirrored in Australian Brassicaceae species, including <i>Stenopetalum</i> and <i>Ballantinia</i> species that diverged ~5.9 Mya (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Mandakova, T., Joly, S., Krzywinski, M., Mummenhoff, K. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Fast diploidization in close mesopolyploid relatives of Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 22, 2277–2290 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1520">22</a>). The relatively unarranged nature of the <i>C. sativa</i> sub-genomes with respect to <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> stands in contrast to these observations but could reflect a more highly conserved nature of these species within the Camelineae tribe. The three sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i>, although showing some differentiation at the nucleotide-level (2–2.5% sequence variation across the coding regions), share a similar gene complement. The hybridization of sub-genomes in <i>C. sativa</i> probably occurred relatively recently, similar to the <i>Brassica</i> crop allopolyploids, resulting in insufficient time for the differentiation of gene complement within the three sub-genomes. Comparisons of pairs of syntelogs found between the three sub-genomes and <i>A. thaliana</i> revealed almost identical <i>K</i>s distributions with a major peak at 0.28 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>), suggesting that the diploid parents of the triplicated <i>C. sativa</i> sub-genomes shared a common ancestor that diverged from <i>A. thaliana</i> ~17 Mya.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Age distribution of duplicated genes in C. sativa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Age distribution of duplicated genes in <i>C. sativa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="440" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Gaussian mixture models fitted to frequency distributions of <i>K</i>s (synonymous substitution) values obtained by comparing pairs of paralogous (<i>C. sativa</i>—neopolyploidy) and orthologous (Sub-genomes 1/2/3 <i>versus A. thaliana</i>) genes are shown. The mixture model analysis is described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 9</a> and the complete list of Gaussian components is provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 23</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Homeologous gene expression bias and genome dominance</h3><p>After polyploidization, duplicated genomes enter an evolutionary trajectory of genetic diploidization during which the nearly identical sub-genomes differentiate via biased loss of homologous genes (fractionation), which is commonly associated with overexpression of genes from the least fractionated sub-genome (genome dominance)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kumar, A. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Plant retrotransposons. Annu. Rev. Genet. 33, 479–532 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1606">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1609">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sankoff, D., Zheng, C. &amp; Zhu, Q. The collapse of gene complement following whole genome duplication. BMC Genomics 11, 313 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1612">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1615">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tang, H. et al. Altered patterns of fractionation and exon deletions in Brassica rapa support a two-step model of paleohexaploidy. Genetics 190, 1563–1574 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1618">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS ONE 7, e36442 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1621">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Langham, R. J. et al. Genomic duplication, fractionation and the origin of regulatory novelty. Genetics 166, 935–945 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1625">28</a></sup>. Preceding genome fractionation is a period of ‘genomic shock’ when a mixture of genetic and epigenetic mechanisms is proposed to lead to neo- or subfunctionalization of duplicated genes.</p><p>Enumeration of syntelogs revealed that the three sub-genomes (Cs-G1, Cs-G2 and Cs-G3) of <i>C. sativa</i> have retained an almost identical number of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 17</a>), which compares starkly with the differentially fractionated LF, MF1 and MF2 sub-genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> that have retained only 13,296, 8,891 and 7,659 genes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). However, it is noteworthy that the rate of gene loss in all three sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> (5% of genes per million year (Myr) is identical to the rate of gene loss in the MF1 sub-genome of <i>B. rapa</i> (5% of genes per Myr; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 18</a>), indicating that the sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> despite lacking a fractionation bias are experiencing the expected exponential decay pattern of gene loss immediately following whole-genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1657">29</a></sup>. Deletion of exonic sequences is one of the mechanisms by which genes are potentially rendered non-functional and subsequently removed from polyploid genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tang, H. et al. Altered patterns of fractionation and exon deletions in Brassica rapa support a two-step model of paleohexaploidy. Genetics 190, 1563–1574 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1661">26</a></sup>. Comparing the coding sequences of homeologous genes within triplicated regions demonstrated that both the number of exons and length of coding sequences in syntelogs were highly conserved (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 19</a>), indicating that limited insertions or deletions have accumulated in coding sequences of <i>C. sativa</i> within the last 5.5 Myrs.</p><p>Gene expression levels are a major determinant of the fate of duplicated copies following whole-genome duplication. It has been shown that the rate of gene loss post polyploidisation may be negatively correlated to the level of gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Gout, J.-F., Kahn, D. &amp; Duret, L. Paramecium Post-Genomics Consortium. The relationship among gene expression, the evolution of gene dosage, and the rate of protein evolution. PLoS Genet. 6, e1000944 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1675">30</a></sup>. Comparison of the expression levels of fully retained and fractionated sets of <i>C. sativa</i> genes revealed a positive correlation between retention rate of triplicated homeologues and their average expression levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a>), suggesting that highly expressed genes tend to persist longer. Evidence of genome dominance in <i>C. sativa</i> was assessed by comparing expression differences between the sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> based on transcript abundance of genes across 12 different tissue types (listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Only the fully retained homeologues were included in this analysis to avoid discrepancy due to fractionation. At sub-genome (G) and tissue-type (T) interaction (G × T) level a statistically significant difference was revealed (<i>P</i>&lt;0.05; ANOVA test for interaction) for 77% (14,391 triplets) of fully retained homeologues. The genes of sub-genome Cs-G3 showed a clear expression level advantage over the other two sub-genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6b</a>), which could result from a two-stage polyploidisation pathway.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Gene expression dynamics reveal genome dominance and functional diversification of C. sativa homeologous genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Gene expression dynamics reveal genome dominance and functional diversification of <i>C. sativa</i> homeologous genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1305" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Relationship between gene retention rate following whole-genome triplication and the gene expression levels. (<b>b</b>) Cumulative frequency of homeologous genes belonging to the three sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i> with highest expression across all tissue types. <i>P</i> values (ANOVA test for interaction, <i>N</i>=108 per gene triplet) were calculated for interaction between sub-genomes (G) and tissue-type (T) effects on expression. To highlight differences between sub-genomes only the subset of the data with <i>P</i>&gt;10<sup>−10</sup> is shown. (<b>c</b>) Scatterplot showing the magnitude of interaction effect calculated as the s.d. from a random effects model estimate for G × T interaction variance. Homeologous triplets were classified into groups, no interaction (<i>P</i>&gt;0.05; ANOVA test for interaction, <i>N</i>=108), negligible interaction (<i>P</i>&lt;0.05 and STDEV(G × T)&lt;0.25) and interaction (<i>P</i>&lt;0.05 and STDEV(G × T)&gt;0.25).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4706/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Only 4,106 of the 14,391 triplets revealed an interaction effect of considerable magnitude (STDEV (G × T) &gt;0.25; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Tab1">Table 1</a>); this set was designated as the ‘interaction group’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6c</a>). Since the differential expression profiles may result from divergent fates of the triplicated genes, further analyses determined the levels of non-functionalization (silencing), neofunctionalization (diversification) or subfunctionalization (shared and/or partitioned functions). Across all 12 tissue types, ~5% of the fully retained homeologues were found to be silenced (FPKM=0 in one or two sub-genomes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Tab1">Table 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 20</a>) with the non-expressed homeologues being equally distributed across all three sub-genomes. Hierarchical clustering of all 12,212 genes belonging to the interaction group based on patterns of gene expression across all 12 tissue types revealed seven major clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Genes belonging to each cluster were predominantly expressed in one or a few tissue types, suggesting potential tissue-specific functions. However, genes from 34% (1,316) of the homeologous triplets clustered together; with the remaining 66% being separated across two (699 triplets) or three (1,904 triplets) independent clusters. As homeologues with altered expression patterns potentially may acquire new or additional functions, the results provide evidence for functional diversification of a subset of triplicated genes within <i>C. sativa</i>. The majority of the triplicated genes (78%) showed no significant differences in either expression levels or tissue specificity, which could impact manipulation of the crop phenotype.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Incidence of expression and functional diversification of fully retained acyl-lipid metabolism related genes in <i>C. sativa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms4706/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Oil metabolism genes</h3><p>In the context of the importance of <i>C. sativa</i> as a biofuel crop, we examined the fractionation and expression divergence of genes encoding proteins and regulatory factors involved in acyl-lipid metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li-Beisson, Y. et al. Acyl-lipid metabolism. Arabidopsis Book 11, e0161 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1966">31</a></sup>. More than 80% of the 736 non-redundant genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li-Beisson, Y. et al. Acyl-lipid metabolism. Arabidopsis Book 11, e0161 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1970">31</a></sup> governing various steps in acyl-lipid biosynthesis, accumulation and degradation were found to be retained in three copies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 21</a>). A subset of the acyl-lipid metabolism genes (26%), mainly involved in fatty acid and triacylglycerol biosynthesis, elongation or degradation, experienced further expansion with a few genes, such as CER1 and WS (both involved in fatty acid elongation and wax biosynthesis) and LOX (involved in oxylipin metabolism), having &gt;10 paralogues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Data 3</a>). The overall expansion of lipid metabolism gene families in <i>C. sativa</i> (217% compared with <i>A. thaliana</i>) is significantly larger than in soybean (63% increase compared with <i>A. thaliana</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d22669e1990">12</a></sup>. Analysis of expression divergence revealed significant differences among only 31% (181 triplets) of the fully retained lipid metabolism genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Hierarchical clustering of genes belonging to this set revealed that only 15% of acyl-lipid metabolism genes were experiencing functional diversification (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The higher retention of lipid metabolism genes in <i>C. sativa</i> does not necessarily reflect adaptation of an oilseed phenotype and could merely be a consequence of polyploidy; however, the larger number of genes involved in lipid metabolism in <i>C. sativa</i>, with the majority showing no expression or functional divergence, suggests that complex regulatory mechanisms govern oil biosynthesis to ensure gene dosage balance. The knowledge of copy number, genomic context and regulation of oil metabolism genes will aid in the future manipulation of biofuel traits in <i>C. sativa.</i></p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p><i>C. sativa</i> is an excellent model that exemplifies the selection of a crop ecotype from a weedy ancestor. Without knowledge of the parental diploids it is difficult to predict the exact origin of <i>C. sativa</i>; however, the strict maintenance of homologous recombination between highly syntenic sub-genomes suggests that like many successful crop species <i>C. sativa</i> may have been formed through inter-specific hybridization of lower chromosome number ancestors. The emerging signatures of genome dominance and functional diversification among a subset of genes in <i>C. sativa</i> are largely concordant with the characteristics of genomic shock triggered by hybridization and dosage imbalance during allopolyploid formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2032">29</a></sup>. The genome sequence strongly supports the hypothesis that the relatively large genome size and high gene content of <i>C. sativa</i> are the consequence of two polyploidy events from an ancestral genome similar to <i>A. lyrata</i>. The minimal chromosomal rearrangements and lack of widespread or biased fractionation in the three <i>C. sativa</i> sub-genomes suggests that the hybridization of the sub-genomes occurred in quick succession and relatively recently, probably emerging on the same time scale as crops such as canola, cotton or wheat, during the rapid expansion of agricultural practices 5–10,000 ya. It is remarkable that despite having nearly identical sub-genomes <i>C. sativa</i> behaves like a diploid with normal disomic inheritance. The age of divergence of the three genomes (5 Mya) may have been sufficient to preclude homeologous pairing, as has been suggested for the natural allopolyploid <i>Arabidopsis suecica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Jackson, S. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Genomic and expression plasticity of polyploidy. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 13, 153–159 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2051">32</a></sup>, or <i>C. sativa</i> similar to wheat and canola may have established the diploid behaviour of the sub-genomes through genetic control of aberrant pairing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Jackson, S. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Genomic and expression plasticity of polyploidy. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 13, 153–159 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2059">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Polyploidization generally evokes a myriad of genetic and epigenetic responses resulting in expression variation and novel regulatory interactions, which is thought to lead to subfunctionalization or neofunctionalization of duplicated genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2066">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Cusack, B. P. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. When gene marriages don’t work out: divorce by subfunctionalization. Trends Genet. 23, 270–272 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2069">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Functional divergence of duplicated genes formed by polyploidy during Arabidopsis evolution. Plant Cell 16, 1679–1691 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2072">34</a></sup>. The genome Cs-G3 shows some evidence of expression dominance and a small proportion of the triplicated genes (22%) suggest functional diversification. However, the overall expression landscape of <i>C. sativa</i> supports preferential sheltering of duplicated genetic material, which would accommodate buffering of essential functions and maintenance of gene dosage balance.</p><p>Polyploidy has commonly been associated with increased allelic diversity, heterozygosity and fixed heterosis, contributing to increased vigour, productivity and novel phenotypic variation, with the resultant prevalence of this phenomenon among crop species. The triplicated gene repertoire of <i>C. sativa</i> may be the genetic basis of several of its desirable agronomic and oil-quality attributes. One of the challenging practical consequences of the homogenous polyploid genetic code in <i>C. sativa</i> is that most traits will be controlled by multiple loci, where both traditional breeding and gene manipulation approaches will be more difficult. However, knowledge of the genome organization of <i>C. sativa</i> combined with ongoing efforts directed towards characterization of its transcriptome and germplasm diversity will accelerate future breeding of elite cultivars and designer oilseed lines for the biofuel and chemical industries.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material and nuclear DNA isolation</h3><p>A homozygous doubled haploid line DH55, derived from <i>C. sativa</i> genotype SRS 933, was chosen for sequencing. For nuclei isolation, ~40 g of fresh leaf tissue from 4-week-old etiolated DH55 seedlings was homogenized in 200 ml ice-cold homogenization buffer (0.01 M Trizma base, 0.08 M KCL, 0.01 M EDTA, 1 mM spermidine, 1 mM spermine, 0.5 M sucrose plus 0.15% β-mercaptoethanol, pH 9.4–9.5). The homogenate was filtered through two layers of cheesecloth and one layer of miracloth, and the nuclei pelleted by centrifugation at 1,800 <i>g</i> at 4 °C for 20 min. The pellet was resuspensed in wash buffer (1 × homogenization buffer plus 0.5% Triton-X100) followed by centrifugation at 1,800 <i>g</i> at 4 °C for 15 min, three times. After the final wash, the nuclei were resuspended in 10 ml lysis buffer (100 mM TrisCl, 100 mM NaCl, 50 mM EDTA, 2% SDS). High-molecular weight genomic DNA was then extracted by traditional proteinase K (0.05 mg ml<sup>−1</sup>; 65 °C for 2 h) digestion followed by RNAase A treatment, two cycles of phenol/chloroform extraction and ethanol precipitation. Quantification of genomic DNA was performed using PicoGreen dsDNA kit (Molecular Probes).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA (5–40 μg) was randomly sheared using the Covaris S2 ultrasonicator (Covaris Inc.), Hydroshear (Genomic Solutions Inc.) or gas-driven nebulizers. For Illumina sequencing, two paired-end (PE) libraries (with median insert sizes of 225 and 325 bps; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 1</a>) and three short-span mate-paired (MP) libraries (3, 5 and 8 Kb) were constructed following standard TruSeq DNA sample preparation and MP library preparation kit v2 (Illumina), respectively. All libraries were size-selected using Pippin prep automated gel electrophoresis system (Sage Science), quantified using a Bioanalyzer (Agilent) and KAPA library quantification kit for Illumina (KAPA Biosystems) and sequenced from both ends (PE) for 100 cycles on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 instrument.</p><p>For 454 pyrosequencing, three medium-span MP libraries with median insert sizes of 15, 20 and 25 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were constructed following the method described in the GS FLX Titanium 20-kb span MP library preparation manual from Roche. Additionally, for 454 pyrosequencing, we constructed a long-span fosmid-based 40 kb MP library using NxSeq 40-kb MP cloning kit (Lucigen) with several modifications to the manufacturer’s protocol. A detailed protocol is described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 6</a>.
