These libraries were sequenced using a Roche 454 FLX Titanium sequencer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome assembly</h3><p>Before assembly, all Illumina and 454 reads were filtered for adapter contamination, PCR duplicates, ambiguous residues (N’s) and low-quality regions, as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 7</a>. The initial backbone of the draft genome was assembled with Illumina reads using <i>De Bruijn</i> graph-based SOAPdenovo (version 2.01) assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2148">35</a></sup>, run with a kmer parameter of 47 (selected after testing a range of kmer values between 31 and 55) and each library ranked according to insert size from smallest to largest. The gaps within assembled scaffolds were filled with the short insert (225 and 325 bp) PE reads using GapCloser (version 1.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2152">35</a></sup>. The resulting assembly consisted of a total of 39,514 contigs and short scaffolds, with a sequence span of 641.39 Mb and an N50 size of 603 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>To improve the scaffold size, we used Bambus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pop, M., Kosack, D. S. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Hierarchical scaffolding with Bambus. Genome Res. 14, 149–159 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2162">36</a></sup> to overlay the MP information generated by 454 pyrosequencing onto SOAPdenovo scaffolds. To achieve this, 454 MP reads of 15–40 kb span were aligned to SOAPdenovo scaffolds using a genomic mapping and alignment programme (GMAP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2166">37</a></sup>. The output from GMAP was used to create a Bambus-compatible GDE-formatted contig file as a source of information about scaffold links. MP links were checked for validity. Redundant or multi-mapped mates were considered invalid; additionally, mates where only one read mapped or if both mates mapped to a single scaffold were also ignored. Thus, only MPs that uniquely mapped against two independent scaffolds with no overlap were considered valid. Bambus was run in a hierarchical fashion (each MP link considered in ascending order of their length) with scaffolding parameters, including redundancy (minimum number of links required to connect two scaffolds) level of 2 and link-size error (estimated error in mate span determination) of 5%. By default, the scaffolds resulting from Bambus are potentially ambiguous as two or more contigs may occupy the same place in the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pop, M., Kosack, D. S. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Hierarchical scaffolding with Bambus. Genome Res. 14, 149–159 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2170">36</a></sup>. Such situations may occur either due to misassembled repeats, or when assembling homeologs within polyploid plant genomes. We used the ‘untangle’ utility of Bambus to disambiguate such scaffolds and generate a collection of linear scaffolds. Bambus was able to order, orient and merge 5,247 of these pre-assembled SOAPdenovo scaffolds into 3,206 superscaffolds, resulting in a greatly improved assembly with an N50 size of 2.16 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">High-density genetic mapping and anchoring of the genome</h3><p>To order, orient and anchor Bambus scaffolds along the chromosome, a high-density genetic map representing 20 linkage groups was constructed using a mapping population (F6) of 96 recombinant inbred lines derived previously from a cross between the phenotypically distinct <i>C. sativa</i> cultivars Lindo and Licalla<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gehringer, A., Friedt, W., Luhs, W. &amp; Snowdon, R. J. Genetic mapping of agronomic traits in false flax (Camelina sativa subsp. sativa). Genome 49, 1555–1563 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2188">1</a></sup>. A total of 3,575 polymorphic loci (SNPs, simple sequence repeats and insertion/deletion polymorphisms; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 3</a>), identified by a combination of the GoldenGate genotyping assay (Illumina) and RAD (Restriction site Associated DNA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Baird, N. A. et al. Rapid SNP discovery and genetic mapping using sequenced RAD markers. PLoS ONE 3, e3376 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2195">38</a></sup> approaches, were used to integrate Bambus superscaffolds with the genetic map.</p><p>To further assist with ordering and orientation of scaffolds for which there was paucity of adequate genetic recombination and markers, collinearity between <i>C. sativa</i> provisional pseudomolecules and <i>A. thaliana</i> chromosomes or <i>A. lyrata</i> scaffolds was established using NUCmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2211">39</a></sup> and BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2215">40</a></sup>. A total of 57 instances of false joins or insertions within Bambus superscaffolds were identified based on marker discontiguity and collinearity information. Such misassembled scaffolds were split and the correct position of each of the fragments was determined based on marker and collinearity information. Final scaffolds were renamed as ‘Scaffold’ and numbered sequentially based on their length from longest to shortest. The order and orientation of scaffolds within each pseudomolecule was determined based on marker order within each scaffold, and marker contiguity pattern between adjoining scaffolds. Scaffolds with too few markers were ordered and oriented using collinearity information. The final version of the draft genome representing 20 pseudochromosomes (corresponding to 20 linkage groups) and 37,398 unanchored scaffolds was collated using a custom Perl script, and the ordering and orientation information of scaffolds within each pseudochromosome was compiled in AGP files. The quality of the assembled genome was ascertained by performing several independent tests, as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Repeat annotation</h3><p>Using the assembled <i>C. sativa</i> genome as input, a <i>de novo</i> repeat library was constructed by using RECON and RepeatScout within RepeatModeler (Version 1.05; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>). To reduce potential false positives, repetitive sequences were compared (BLASTX with E-value cutoff of 1E−5) with annotated gene models in the <i>A. thaliana</i> protein database and significant non-TE hits were removed. The final consensus repeat library was used to mask the genome by RepeatMasker (Version 3.3.0; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene annotation</h3><p>For accurate annotation of gene models, an integrated computational approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>) based on two major annotation pipelines, Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2266">41</a></sup> and PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2270">42</a></sup>, was adopted. Maker provides a simplified process for aligning ESTs and proteins to the genome, and integrates this external homology evidence with <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions to produce final gene annotations with evidence-based quality statistics. Inputs for Maker included the repeat-masked <i>C. sativa</i> genome assembly, 42,350 ESTs, a genome-guided <i>de novo</i> transcript assembly comprising 201,365 transcripts and a protein database containing annotated proteins from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>Thellungiella parvula</i>. <i>Ab initio</i> gene predictions were made by Fgenesh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2300">43</a></sup> and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Stanke, M., Tzvetkova, A. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome. Genome Biol. 7, S11 (11–18) (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2304">44</a></sup>. Maker gene structure annotations were further updated by PASA using evidence from <i>de novo</i> RNA-seq assembly and Sanger/454 ESTs. Annotation updates by PASA included annotation of untranslated regions, addition of models for alternative splicing variants and gene boundary adjustments. A total of 84,071 genes were annotated by this approach, of which 7,175 genes were identified as ‘fused’ where two or more neighbouring <i>A. thaliana</i> genes aligned (BLASTN with E-value cutoff of 1E−10) to different parts of a single predicted gene model in <i>C. sativa</i>. The fused genes were replaced with 12,984 alternative gene models and the output was passed through another round of PASA. By manual curation, 793 EST-only or other predictions that overlapped with gene models that had better external homology evidence support were removed. The final annotation set contained a total of 89,418 genes encoding 94,495 transcripts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Synteny analysis</h3><p>Sequence homology was detected by BLASTP of the predicted proteins against <i>A. thaliana</i> proteome. BLAST hits with E-value of 1e−20 or better and within the top 40% drop from the best bit score were kept for further analysis. The chains of syntenic <i>C. sativa</i>–<i>A. thaliana</i> gene pairs were computed by DAGChainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2334">45</a></sup> using default parameters. In case of a <i>C. sativa</i> gene participating in more than one syntenic chain due to duplication in the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome, the <i>C. sativa</i>–<i>A. thaliana</i> pair in the weaker scoring chain was removed from the analysis. The syntelog table was generated by placing the syntenic chains onto the <i>C. sativa</i> chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Reconstruction of triplicated sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i></h3><p>Considering the high level of conservation of synteny and GB contiguity between <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> species, the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome was utilized to represent the genome organization of individual sub-genomes within <i>C. sativa</i>. Since the prevalence of inversions and intrachromosomal rearrangements is thought to be more common than interchromosomal translocations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2377">23</a></sup>, segments of each <i>C. sativa</i> chromosome syntenic to corresponding <i>A. lyrata</i> chromosomes were assumed to be derived from the same ancestral chromosome. In the event where interchromosomal rearrangements were inferred, the order, orientation and contiguity of genes across potential adjacent segments were examined, and the most parsimonious scenario for the original segment order and chromosome assignment within each sub-genome was deduced.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>The whole-plant transcriptome of <i>C. sativa</i> based on Illumina RNA-seq data was characterized to assist in the genome annotation process. Twelve different tissue samples were collected during both vegetative (germinating seed, cotyledon, young leaf, senescing leaf, root and stem) and reproductive (bud, flower, and early, early-mid, late-mid and late seed development) stages of the life cycle (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 10</a>). For each tissue type, at least three independent biological replicates were analysed. Total RNA from vegetative tissue was isolated using the RNeasy plant mini kit (Qiagen), including on-column DNase digestion, according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Total RNA from siliques was isolated using a method described by Suzuki <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Suzuki, Y., Kawazu, T. &amp; Koyama, H. RNA isolation from siliques, dry seeds, and other tissues of Arabidopsis thaliana. Biotechniques 37, 544 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2404">46</a></sup>, consisting of a two-step extraction process with high sodium extraction buffer isopropanol precipitation and LiCl precipitation, and then cleaned using RNeasy plant Mini kit (Qiagen), including on-column DNase digestion. The integrity and quantity of total RNA was assessed using RNA 6000 Nano labchip on the BioAnalyzer (Agilent). Sequencing libraries were constructed following standard TruSeq RNA sample preparation guide (Illumina) and multiplexed (12 samples per lane of a flow cell), PE sequencing was performed using the Illumina Hiseq 2000 platform. A total of 78.5 Gb raw RNA-seq data was generated. Before assembly, all reads were filtered for adapter contamination, ambiguous residues (N’s) and low quality regions, as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 7</a>. <i>De novo</i> assembly of transcripts and expression analysis was performed using a combination of different programs, including Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2415">47</a></sup>, Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2419">47</a></sup>, Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2423">48</a></sup> and PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2427">42</a></sup>.</p><p>Clean and non-ribosomal reads from one biological replicate of each tissue sample were pooled and used for genome-guided <i>de novo</i> transcript assembly. A hybrid approach combining three different programs, including Tophat, Trinity and PASA, was employed to align RNA-seq reads to the genome (Tophat), assemble aligned reads (Trinity) and further align and assemble the Trinity-reconstructed transcripts (PASA). This approach produced 201,365 transcripts, which were used in the annotation of protein-coding genes in the <i>C. sativa</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><p>For <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly, clean and non-ribosomal reads from all tissue samples (including all three biological replicates) were pooled. <i>De novo</i> assembly was carried out using Trinity with default parameters. The final assembly included 271,745 Trinity transcripts and 115,114 Trinity components, which were used for updating Maker gene structure annotation using PASA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><p>To estimate transcript abundance, the whole-plant and tissue-wise expression of <i>C. sativa</i> genes was assessed using a Tophat and Cufflink-based method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2461">47</a></sup>. For both Tophat and Cufflink analysis, default parameters were used except that the maximum and minimum intron lengths were set at 2,500 and 20, respectively. For whole-plant-wide expression analysis of <i>C. sativa</i> genes, RNA-seq data from all tissues and biological replicates were pooled. Transcript abundance was measured as fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped (FPKM) values.</p><p>For all the 18,565 fully retained <i>Camelina sativa</i> genes, the raw expression values (FPKM) from each of the 12 tissue types were transformed by adding 1 and taking the natural logarithm, in order to analyse expression divergence. All subsequent calculations were done with the transformed values. This transformation reduces the range of the data, and the residuals more frequently follow a normal distribution (determined by the distribution of <i>P</i> values for the Shapiro–Wilk test for normality), which is an assumption of ANOVA. Of the gene triplets, 18,491 had non-zero variance of expression. For the expression analyses, the mean was taken over the three replicates, for each sub-genome of origin (G) and tissue type (T) combination. The mean for each G was also obtained (over all T and replicates). Two-way ANOVAs were carried out to test for the interaction between the G effect and the T effect on expression in addition to the sum of these separate effects. The cumulative frequency of each genome demonstrating the highest expression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6b</a>) was obtained after sorting the gene triplets into increasing order of <i>P</i> value (ANOVA test for interaction, based on a sample size of 108 per gene triplet (3 genes by 12 tissue types by 3 replicates)) for the G × T effect. Of the 18,491 gene triplets, 14,391 showed a significant (<i>P</i>&lt;0.05; ANOVA test for interaction) G × T interaction effect. However, in most cases due to small statistical error the interaction was small in magnitude despite its statistical significance. The magnitude of interaction <i>σ</i><sub>i</sub> was estimated as the square root of the variance of the interaction in the random effects model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Montgomery, D. C. Design and Analysis of Experiments 3rd edn John Wiley and Sons Inc. (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms4706#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d22669e2492">49</a></sup> that considers the levels of G and T as randomly sampled. This estimate was obtained from equation (1)</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw317/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4706/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4706_Equ1_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt="" /></div></div><p>where <i>E</i> stands for expectation, and the expected MS terms are the expected mean squares calculated as in ANOVA, and <i>n</i> is the number of replicates (in this case 3). The scatterplot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#Fig6">Fig. 6c</a>) illustrates the relationship between these two measures of the interaction. Only 4,106 triplets are both statistically significant (<i>P</i>&lt;0.05; ANOVA test for interaction) and have a magnitude of interaction <i>σ</i><sub>i</sub>&gt;0.25. From this set, only the individual genes (12,112) that had a non-zero expression for at least one tissue type were considered for hierarchical clustering. The clustering was carried out using 1 minus the Pearson sample correlation as the distance measure, and this requires the sample variances of the means for each gene to be non-zero. These sample variances were only zero in the case that all the means were zero. The average linkage method was used for clustering. This method clusters correlated sets of expression means together regardless of the absolute magnitude of the expression levels. Using this clustering, the heatmap (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>) was drawn using the statistical package R where the colours were 256 levels of rainbow, starting with red representing low values and ending with blue representing high values. The superimposed pink-coloured rectangles highlight areas of low or high expression. The breakpoints determining the <i>y</i> coordinates of the rectangles were also chosen with reference to the dendrogram for the clustering such that they separated clearly defined clusters of genes having similar expression patterns.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Identification of orphan genes</h3><p>A blast-based filtering approach was used to identify orphan genes in the <i>C. sativa</i> genome. BLASTP (E-value cutoff of 0.01) was used to search all predicted peptides of <i>C. sativa</i> against all 39 sequenced plant species available at phytozome.net (v9.1) excluding five Brassicaceae species. The orphan candidates were filtered out, and then BLAST searched against the NCBI nr, nt and est databases (E-value cutoff of 0.01). Orphans having significant blast hits with Brassicaceae species were filtered out. The species and family information of all the candidates were extracted from the NCBI taxonomy database using in-house developed scripts. Filtered candidates were further searched against the nr database using PSI-BLAST. Orphan candidates displaying InterProScan hits with non-Brassicaceae species were not considered as orphans. <i>C. sativa</i> specific orphans were extracted from the 3,761 Brassicaceae-specific orphans by BLAST search of these genes against the <i>C. sativa</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Identification of evolutionary origins of orphan genes</h3><p><i>C. sativa</i> orphan genes originating by gene duplication events were identified by BLASTP and BLASTN searches against all non-orphan <i>C. sativa</i> genes. Orphan genes containing non-coding or out-of-frame CDS hits were identified using BLASTN against all sequenced plant species, and hits with Brassicaceae and non-Brassicaceae species were categorized. An orphan gene was considered to be overprinted if the peptide sequence of orphan overlapped with the CDSs of other genes (not with untranslated regions or intronic regions).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">BAC library construction and sequencing</h3><p>A ~10-fold coverage BAC library of <i>C. sativa</i> was constructed in the pIndiogoBAC vector by Bio S&amp;T Inc. (Montreal, Canada). Young etiolated leaves from seedlings of DH55 were used as source material for BAC library construction. A subset of 768 randomly selected BACs was sequenced by Amplicon Express Inc. (Pullman, WA, USA) by Focused Genome Sequencing, a next-generation sequencing-based method that allows high-quality assembly of BAC clone sequence data using the Illumina platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Phylogenetic analysis of <i>Camelina</i> species</h3><p><i>De novo</i> transcriptome sequencing of five <i>Camelina</i> species, including <i>C. hispida, C. rumelica</i> ssp. transcaspica, <i>C. rumelica</i> ssp. Iran, <i>C. rumelica</i> ssp. USSR and <i>C. laxa</i>, was performed using Roche 454 pyrosequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Table 22</a>). The unigene sets generated by <i>de novo</i> EST assembly were utilized in establishing a highly resolved molecular phylogeny of <i>Camelina</i> species and their relationship with the three sub-genomes of <i>C. sativa</i>. Additional information is provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4706#MOESM101">Supplementary Note 5</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec25" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec25-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec25">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec25-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> Sequence data for <i>Camelina sativa</i> have been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank sequence read archive under the accession codes <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP038024">SRP038024</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRS558774">SRS558774</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRS566487">SRS566487</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRS559344">SRS559344</a>. The genome assembly for <i>Camelina sativa</i> has been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database under the accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=JFZQ00000000">JFZQ00000000</a>.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Kagale, S. <i>et al.</i> The emerging biofuel crop <i>Camelina sativa</i> retains a highly undifferentiated hexaploid genome structure. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 5:3706 doi: 10.1038/ncomms4706 (2014).</p></div></div></section>
